    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="801ae81546924c63b2eeab7bbbe2a476">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '2623e946-209c-4b2b-a775-933ad250b015'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

            function riskifiedBeaconLoad() {

   <div class="abstract-group  metis-abstract">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" id="abstract-graphical-en">
         <h2 id="d203336085" class="article-section__header section__title graphical abstractlang_en graphical">Graphical Abstract</h2>
         <div class="graphical-abstract">
            <p>A concept of customized strategy via multiple rock-forming elements trace doping is presented to solve the stability–capacity trade-off dilemma of layered oxide for high-voltage sodium-ion batteries. The designed Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.28</sub>Mg<sub>0.04</sub>Al<sub>0.02</sub>Mn<sub>0.64</sub>Ti<sub>0.02</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode exhibits high energy density approaching 500 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, with good capacity retention of 81.2 % after 200 cycles. The full cell delivers a state-of-the-art energy density of 303.3 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>.
            </p><figure>
               <div class="center graphic__image"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/3a92888b-ebd6-46d1-9c64-c92060bfb636/anie202411618-toc-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/3a92888b-ebd6-46d1-9c64-c92060bfb636/anie202411618-toc-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/aa79fa52-bedb-433d-8f05-c3b5dbef6a2b/anie202411618-toc-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/3a92888b-ebd6-46d1-9c64-c92060bfb636/anie202411618-toc-0001-m.jpg" alt="Description unavailable" title="Description unavailable" loading="lazy"></picture></a><p class="caption-style"></p>
               </div>
            </figure>
            <p></p>
            </div>
      </section>
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d203336091" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>High-voltage P2-Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> layered oxide cathode exhibits significant potential for sodium-ion batteries, owing to the elevated operating voltage and theoretical energy density beyond lithium iron phosphate, but the large-volume phase transition is the devil. Currently, this type cathode still suffers from stability–capacity trade-off dilemma. Herein, a concept of customized strategy via multiple rock-forming elements trace doping is presented to address the mentioned issue. The customized Mg-Al-Ti trace doped cathode maintains a notable capacity of 140.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> with an energy density approaching 500 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, and shows good cycle stability, retaining 89.0 % of its capacity after 50 cycles at 0.1 C. Additionally, the full cell, paired with a hard carbon anode, achieves an advanced energy density of 303.3 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>. The multiple characterizations reveal the failure mechanism of contrast sample involving severe intragranular cracks coupled with layer to rock salt transformation, which reduces active substance and increases charge transfer resistance. The doped sample with increased sliding energy barrier well suppresses this phenomenon. Impressively, the customized strategy can be extended to Mg-Fe-Ti system. This research provides a novel concept for the design of high energy sodium-ion cathode.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202411618-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202411618-sec-0001-title"> Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) have shown great potential as an alternative to lithium-ion batteries (LIBs) for applying to large-scale energy storage system, due to the features of globally abundant sodium reserves and low-cost.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411618-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0001_R_d203336070e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Science">1</a>, <a href="#anie202411618-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0002_R_d203336070e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Nat. Energy">2</a></sup></span> However, the heavier Na<sup>+</sup> and higher redox potential of Na<sup>+</sup>/Na result in low energy density, compared to the Li counterpart.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411618-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0003_R_d203336070e542" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Adv. Energy Mater.">3</a>, <a href="#anie202411618-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0004_R_d203336070e542" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Energy Environ. Sci.">4</a></sup></span> In this content, the low energy density of SIBs induced relative high cost in whole device still restricts the practical application, although the SIBs possess the potentially low-cost feature. The energy density of SIBs depends on both cathode and anode. Currently, the electrochemical performances of hard carbon anode are close to and even better than the commercial graphite anode for LIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411618-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0005_R_d203336070e545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">5</a>, <a href="#anie202411618-bib-0006" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0006_R_d203336070e545" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 6 - Adv. Mater.">6</a></sup></span> Unfortunately, the cathode side becomes the rate-determining step limiting the energy density, because the developed cathode materials are far from the commercial lithium iron phosphate cathode.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411618-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0007_R_d203336070e549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">7</a>, <a href="#anie202411618-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0008_R_d203336070e549" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Adv. Energy Mater.">8</a></sup></span> Besides, the cathode materials also account for the main cost of SIBs device. Therefore, developing high energy cathode materials with low-cost feature is an essential step to push forward the commercialization of SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>Among the diverse cathode materials, layered oxides, featuring high energy density, facile preparation, and low cost, are deemed as one of the most potential candidates.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411618-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0009_R_d203336070e555" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">9</a></sup></span> These oxides can be categorized into P or O type structures based on the coordination of Na<sup>+</sup> ions and the stacking sequence of oxygen layers.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411618-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0010_R_d203336070e560" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - Physica B+C">10</a></sup></span> It is generally accepted that the P type structures always exhibit better ion-diffusion kinetics and air-stability, due to the low sodium-ion diffusion barrier of face to face Na<sup>+</sup> conduction and high redox potential.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411618-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0011_R_d203336070e565" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Nat. Energy">11</a>, <a href="#anie202411618-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0012_R_d203336070e565" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Adv. Energy Mater.">12</a></sup></span> Particularly, the typical P2-Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NNMO) cathode material has been considered as a hopeful cathode, due to its promising characteristics: a high theoretical capacity of 173 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, an average voltage exceeding 3.6 V, and an energy density surpassing 600 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, which exceeds that of lithium iron phosphate.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411618-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0013_R_d203336070e582" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">13</a>, <a href="#anie202411618-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0014_R_d203336070e582" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Chem. Soc. Rev.">14</a></sup></span> However, the suffered phase transition of P2-O2 at high de-sodiated state induces large volume variation of ~22 %, which leads to fast capacity and voltage decay, limiting the practical application.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411618-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0013_R_d203336070e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - J. Electrochem. Soc.">13</a>, <a href="#anie202411618-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0015_R_d203336070e585" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Mater.">15</a></sup></span> Ion doping is widely acknowledged as an effective strategy for suppressing phase transitions and enhancing cycle performance in battery materials. For instance, Wang et al. employed Mg as a lightweight dopant to replace some nickel, successfully suppressing phase transitions and achieving a commendable reversible capacity of 123 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411618-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0016_R_d203336070e590" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">16</a></sup></span> In another study, Peng et al. reported a dual-site doped [Na<sub>0.67</sub>Zn<sub>0.05</sub>]Ni<sub>0.18</sub>Cu<sub>0.1</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> material, demonstrating exceptional cycle stability with 97.2 % capacity retention after 100 cycles at 0.1 C, albeit with a reduced reversible specific capacity of 106 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411618-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0015_R_d203336070e608" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Mater.">15</a></sup></span> Recently, Kubota and co-workers developed Na<sub>2/3</sub>[Ni<sub>1/4</sub>Mn<sub>1/2</sub>Ti<sub>1/6</sub>Zn<sub>1/12</sub>]O<sub>2</sub>, utilizing dual substitution to achieve quasi-solid-solution reaction, resulting in robust cycle performance and a suitable reversible capacity of 114 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411618-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0017_R_d203336070e627" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Mater.">17</a></sup></span> It can be seen that this material still faces the stability–capacity trade-off dilemma, as demonstrated in Table S1. Up to now, achieving stable cycle of cathode materials with a high energy density of approaching 500 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> for SIBs is still a big challenge,<span><sup><a href="#anie202411618-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0018_R_d203336070e632" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">18</a>-<a href="#anie202411618-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0020_R_d203336070e632" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Adv. Energy Mater.">20</a></sup></span> which is comparable to the commercial LiFePO<sub>4</sub>. In this content, it is required that this class of positive electrode materials need to achieve a high actual capacity of 140 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> as well as preserving the high voltage feature. Therefore, an elaborate component regulation should be well designed, which should largely maintain the active species and also achieve an excellent structural stability. Recently, the multi-elements high-entropy doping with increased configurational entropy can achieve the effect of “small investment and big benefit”, which could break the stability–capacity trade-off dilemma.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411618-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0021_R_d203336070e640" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Nature">21</a></sup></span> Hence, implanting this concept into NNMO cathode material to realize “cost decreasing and benefit increasing” is a predictably useful strategy.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, we present a customized strategy by multiple rock-forming elements trace doping to tackle the stability–capacity trade-off dilemma of NNMO cathode material, achieving stable cycle of cathode with notable energy density approaching 500 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>. According to the resource abundance (Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0001">1a</a>) and functions,<span><sup><a href="#anie202411618-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0022_R_d203336070e651" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Storage Mater.">22</a>-<a href="#anie202411618-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0025_R_d203336070e651" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - ACS Cent. Sci.">25</a></sup></span> the low-cost rock forming elements of Al, Fe, Mg, Ti are suitable as doping ions. As a prototype of concept, a customized high performance Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.28</sub>Mg<sub>0.04</sub>Al<sub>0.02</sub>Mn<sub>0.64</sub>Ti<sub>0.02</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (denoted as MAT-NNMO) with theoretical capacity ~145 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> based on the active species of nickel is designed. Benefiting from the synergistic effects of multiple ions and entropy stabilization, the MAT-NNMO cathode demonstrates a remarkable reversible capacity of 140.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and high working voltage of 3.474 V, leading to an impressive energy density of 487.4 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>. Furthermore, it exhibits good cycle stability, retaining 89.0 % of its capacity after 50 cycles at 0.1 C, which is twice as high as that of the NNMO sample. Experimental characterizations combined with density functional theory (DFT) calculations highlight that the enhanced electrochemical properties arise from elevated sliding energy barriers of transition metal layer, suppressed phase transitions, fewer formation of rock-salt structure, and improved kinetic behavior.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202411618-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/344daaa7-9822-4677-94ab-1dba11b20db6/anie202411618-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>

      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202411618-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202411618-sec-0002-title"> Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The NNMO and MAT-NNMO were synthesized via a typical sol-gel method, see experimental section for detail. The phase information was first studied by X-ray diffraction (XRD), as depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0001">1b</a>,<a href="#anie202411618-fig-0001">c</a>. All samples exhibit well-indexed patterns corresponding to the P2 phase with a <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i> space group by Rietveld refinement. The refined results were provided in Table S3 and S4. The lattice parameters for NNMO are <i>a</i>=<i>b</i>=2.8875 Å, <i>c</i>=11.1563, and V=80.5580 Å<sup>3</sup>. The lattice parameters for MAT-NNMO are <i>a</i>=<i>b</i>=2.8896 Å, <i>c</i>=11.1610 Å, and V=80.7060 Å<sup>3</sup>. The increased lattice parameters for MAT-NNMO are followed to the Vegard's law due to the overall larger ion radius of doping ions.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411618-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0026_R_d203336070e742" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Phys. Rev. A">26</a></sup></span> The crystal structure diagram is shown in Figure S1, in which the structure is formed by ordering stack of TMO<sub>2</sub> and NaO<sub>2</sub> layers (TM means transition metal). Field emission scanning electron microscope (FESEM) was used to investigate the typical morphological feature. As shown in Figure S2, all samples demonstrate plate-like morphology, while the MAT-NNMO exhibits smaller particle size. The plate-like morphology is also confirmed by transmission electron microscope (TEM), as depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0001">1d</a>. The microstructure information of MAT-NNMO was further surveyed through high-resolution TEM (HRTEM). As illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0001">1e</a>, the HRTEM and corresponding fast Fourier transformation (FFT) pattern indicate the two-dimensional lattice fringes, which can be ascribed to (100) and (010) planes of P2 type structure with a space group of <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub>/<i>mmc</i>. In addition, the single crystalline selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern (Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0001">1f</a>) further confirms the good crystallinity of MAT-NNMO with a hexagonal structure. Interestingly, the SAED pattern exhibits an obviously in-plane transition metal ordering structure, coupled with ordering dark spots along with the bright diffraction spots. As shown in Figure S3, the XRD refinement results further confirm the existed in-plane ordering structure. Therefore, in order to better illustrate the diffraction information, we adopt a new structure with a space group of <i>P</i>6<sub>3</sub> through considering the in-plane transition metal ordering. The four main diffraction spots are indexed to (2–10), (110), (1–10), and (100) planes. The energy dispersive X-ray spectroscopy (EDS) mappings (Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0001">1g</a>) verify the successful doping of Mg, Al, Ti elements, and all the elements are distributed uniformly.</p>
         
         <p>The electrochemical characteristics of these two samples were examined to assess the impact of trace doping with multiple elements. The electrochemical tests were carried out within the voltage window of 2.0–4.35 V. Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0002">2a</a> and <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0002">2b</a> depict the charge–discharge profiles during the initial 50 cycles of NNMO and MAT-NNMO for comparison. Observations indicate that the MAT-NNMO cathode shows smoother curves, which was also confirmed by the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curve (Figure S4). Besides, the initial CV curves for MAT-NNMO exhibit excellent overlap with minimal overpotential, while the NNMO sample is worse, indicating the potentially improved kinetics in MAT-NNMO. Obviously, the NNMO exhibits fast capacity and voltage decay, while the MAT-NNMO well alleviates this phenomenon. As a comparison (Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0002">2c</a>), the MAT-NNMO exhibits a high initial reversible specific capacity of 140.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, maintaining 89.0 % capacity after 50 cycles at 0.1 C, while the NNMO only preserves 44.8 % of its initial specific capacity. As shown in Figure S5a, the MAT-NNMO also exhibits slower voltage decay rate with 0.076 % per cycle, which is much lower than the NNMO (0.33 %). The suppressed voltage and capacity decay through multiple elements trace doping significantly improves the maintenance of energy density with a retention rate of 85.5 % after 50 cycles, which is two times higher than the NNMO electrode (37.4 %, Figure S5b). Furthermore, the MAT-NNMO also demonstrates better rate performance, as depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0002">2d</a>. The charge–discharge profiles for rate performances, presented in Figure S6, indicate that MAT-NNMO exhibits better reaction kinetics. Notably, even under high-rate conditions up to 5 C, the high-voltage plateau (beyond 4.2 V) corresponding to the poor kinetics region can also be partly activated in MAT-NNMO cathode, while this plateau is disappeared in NNMO electrode just at 1 C rate. What's more, the MAT-NNMO also exhibits higher Coulombic efficiency (Figure S7). In order to Figure out the kinetic difference between NNMO and MAT-NNMO, galvanostatic intermittent titration technique (GITT), considered as one of the most powerful tools to calculate diffusion coefficient, was carried out. As shown in Figure S8, the MAT-NNMO obviously demonstrates higher Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics, especially at the high voltage plateau, which is nearly 2–3 orders of magnitude higher than the NNMO sample. In addition, we also investigate the electronic conductivity for both samples by DC polarization method. As shown in Figure S9a,b, the results demonstrate the enhanced electronic conductivity in MAT-NNMO. The enhanced electronic conductivity can be attributed to the altered electronic structure after trace doping, which reduces the band gap (Figure S9c,d). Therefore, the both improved ionic conductivity and electronic conductivity in MAT-NNMO account for the enhanced rate performance. The long cycle stability of MAT-NNMO was investigated under 2 C, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0002">2e</a>. The MAT-NNMO demonstrates a notable reversible capacity of 113.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and maintains good cycle stability, retaining 81.2 % of its capacity after 200 cycles, highlighting its practical applicability. To further investigate the impact of multiple element trace doping, we also synthesized three samples with single element doping, including Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.25</sub>Mg<sub>0.08</sub>Mn<sub>0.67</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (named M-NNMO), Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.33</sub>Mn<sub>0.59</sub>Ti<sub>0.08</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (named T-NNMO), and Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.29</sub>Al<sub>0.08</sub>Mn<sub>0.63</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (named A-NNMO). The phase structure of these samples was investigated by XRD and corresponding Le Bail refinement, as shown in Figure S10 and Table S5–S7. It is shown that the Mg and Ti doped sample exhibit pure P2 phase, while the Al doped sample demonstrates a P2&amp;P3 biphasic structure. The electrochemical performance (Figure S11) shows that all the doped samples exhibit positive impact on improving the cycling stability compared to NNMO sample, but the single element doped samples also show poor performance compared with the MAT-NNMO, which highlights the critical effect of multiple element trace doping. In order to extend the feasibility of customized strategy, the multiple component trace doped Na<sub>0.67</sub>Ni<sub>0.28</sub>Mg<sub>0.04</sub>Fe<sub>0.02</sub>Ti<sub>0.02</sub>Mn<sub>0.64</sub>O<sub>2</sub> cathode material (denoted as MFT-NNMO) was also successfully synthesized (Figure S12). The MFT-NNMO can also exhibit high initial reversible specific capacity of 138.1 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, corresponding to a high energy density of 490.1 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>. It improves the rate and cycle properties as well, as illustrated in Figure S13.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202411618-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e5b80fe4-a9a4-4545-9180-57ce55e5b385/anie202411618-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/e5b80fe4-a9a4-4545-9180-57ce55e5b385/anie202411618-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ad8e8135-9320-406d-b734-2f45c68e1f28/anie202411618-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e5b80fe4-a9a4-4545-9180-57ce55e5b385/anie202411618-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202411618-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202411618" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>Charge-discharge profiles of (a) NNMO and (b) MAT-NNMO at 0.1 C (1 C=170 mA g<sup>−1</sup>) during 50 cycles. Comparison of (c) cycle stability and (d) rate performance. (e) Cycle stability of MAT-NNMO at 2 C. (f) Charge-discharge curves of MAT-NNMO//HC. (g) The cycle stability at 0.2 C. (h) Comparison of energy density between this work and previously advanced works.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To further demonstrate practical applicability, a coin-type sodium-ion full cell was assembled using MAT-NNMO as the cathode and commercial hard carbon (HC) as the anode, consistent with previous methodologies.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411618-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0027_R_d203336070e878" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - ACS Materials Lett.">27</a></sup></span> The cathode and anode were carefully matched for charge balance in the full cell configuration, maintaining a mass ratio of active cathode to anode at 2 : 1. Charge-discharge curves of the full cell were recorded at 0.1 C and 0.2 C rates (based on the active cathode material) within a voltage range of 1.9–4.3 V, as depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0002">2f</a>. At 0.1 C, the full cell achieved a high specific capacity of 129.0 mAh g<sub>cathode</sub><sup>−1</sup>. Furthermore, stable cycling at 0.2 C revealed an initial capacity of 112 mAh g<sub>cathode</sub><sup>−1</sup> and an impressive capacity retention of 91.6 % after 50 cycles (Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0002">2g</a>). The energy density of the full cell was calculated based on the total mass of active cathode and anode materials. As illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0002">2h</a>, the full cell demonstrated an exceptional energy density of 303.3 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> and maintained stable cycling with a high energy density of 258.8 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>, marking significant progress compared to previously reported advanced SIBs.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411618-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0015_R_d203336070e901" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Adv. Mater.">15</a>, <a href="#anie202411618-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0022_R_d203336070e901" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Energy Storage Mater.">22</a>, <a href="#anie202411618-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0028_R_d203336070e901" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Adv. Mater.">28</a>-<a href="#anie202411618-bib-0037" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0037_R_d203336070e901" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 37 - Nat. Sustain.">37</a></sup></span></p>
         
         <p>In situ XRD was performed on NNMO and MAT-NNMO in order to investigate the structure evolution during Na<sup>+</sup> de-intercalation and intercalation process. The detailed configuration and assembly of in situ cell were illustrated in Figure S14. As shown in Figure S15 and S16, all the diffractions for the cathodes are moved regularly during charge–discharge process, while the peak position and intensity of diffractions that do not shift are ascribed to the cell part or Al foil. The contour maps of 14–38° with three main diffraction peaks of (002), (004), and (100) for NNMO and MAT-NNMO were shown in Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0003">3a</a>,<a href="#anie202411618-fig-0003">b</a>. During charge–discharge process, (002) and (004) diffraction peaks in both samples first shift to lower 2θ and then recover to higher angle, while the (100) peaks first move to higher angle and then restore to the initial state. Unfortunately, as for the NNMO cathode, a new diffraction peak corresponding to O2 phase at about 20° emerges during the high-voltage plateau, which exhibits obviously P2−O2 phase transition, leading to great lattice shrinkage along the <i>c</i> axis. Interestingly, the P2−O2 phase transition is well suppressed in MAT-NNMO and a Z phase at nearly 17° is formed.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411618-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0038_R_d203336070e917" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Energy Environ. Sci.">38</a></sup></span> The phase transition is clear observed at the fully charged state, as depicted in Figure S17. In addition, the difference in homogeneous electrochemical reaction reflected in the first 20<sup>th</sup> in situ XRD patterns (Figure S18) indicate the more inhomogeneous electrochemical reaction in NNMO, which may induce lattice stress and is harmful to electrochemical performance. In order to demonstrate the difference between O2 phase and Z phase, the changes of lattice parameters at different Na<sup>+</sup> removal contents are calculated from the in situ XRD data. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0003">3c</a>,<a href="#anie202411618-fig-0003">d</a>, the lattice parameter of a in both samples is gradually reduced with similar evolution tendency. However, when the sodium content was about 0.3 in bulk, the NNMO with P2−O2 phase transition displays large shrink of lattice parameter c, while the MAT-NNMO with P2−Z phase transition show a smaller variation. The lattice parameters at the first occurrence of phase transition were collected for both samples, as highlighted in Figure S19. The standard diffraction peaks obtained by adopting the calculated lattice parameter acquired via the Bragg equation are consistent with the experimental result (Figure S20). The volume variations of phase transitions indicate the smaller change of MAT-NNMO (4.57 %) than the NNMO (22 %), which is also indicated in Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0003">3e</a>. Overall, due to the sliding of TMO<sub>2</sub> along the <i>a-b</i> planes in NNMO electrode during deeply de-sodiated state, it exhibits a P2−O2 phase transition with a large volume shrinkage of ~22 % (Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0003">3f</a>), which deteriorates the structure stability and lowers the diffusion kinetics, leading to poor cycle and rate performance. As a comparison, the MAT-NNMO (Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0003">3g</a>) effectively suppresses the sliding of TMO<sub>2</sub> and exhibits a small volume variation, contributing to better electrochemical properties.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202411618-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/5ecbab3f-611f-4642-a01d-12a0262bf6b6/anie202411618-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/5ecbab3f-611f-4642-a01d-12a0262bf6b6/anie202411618-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/4e649788-bb28-45e8-a038-deade8a5cb20/anie202411618-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/5ecbab3f-611f-4642-a01d-12a0262bf6b6/anie202411618-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202411618-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202411618" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>The in situ XRD of (a) NNMO and (b) MAT-NNMO during first cycle (Time scale bar is 100 min). (c, d) The corresponding variation of lattice parameters at different Na de-intercalation content. Lattice parameters a and c of P2 solid solution and two-phase coexistence were presented at the region covered by green and orange rectangle, respectively. Lattice parameters a were represented by red open circles, lattice parameters c of P2 were represented by blue triangles, and lattice parameters c of O2 and Z were represented by blue open triangles in (c) and (d), respectively. (e) The calculated volume as a function of sodium content in both samples. Schematic illustration of phase transition for (f) NNMO and (g) MAT-NNMO.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>For the purpose of figuring out the effect of phase transition on the microstructure and clarifying its relationship with electrochemical properties, the microstructure of post-cycled electrode materials from the micron scale to the nanometer scale were thoroughly investigated. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0004">4a</a>, the SEM image of pristine NNMO sample shows that each plate demonstrates tight structure without any cracks, while the post-cycled NNMO particles after 50 cycles at 0.1 C display severe intragranular cracks (Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0004">4b</a>). The occurring intragranular cracks will accelerate the permeation of electrolyte and side reactions, resulting in rapid loss of active substances and poor electrochemical performance. As a comparison, no cracks generation in both pristine and post-cycled MAT-NNMO, except a little rough surface is detected in post-cycled MAT-NNMO, as depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0004">4c</a>,<a href="#anie202411618-fig-0004">d</a>. In addition, the randomly selected SEM images (Figure S21) of post-cycled cathodes also confirm that this phenomenon is a common situation, validating the robust structure of MAT-NNMO. The TEM was used to observe the structural evolution at nanometer or sub-nano scale. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0004">4e</a>,<a href="#anie202411618-fig-0004">f</a>, the pristine NNMO material viewed from a direction perpendicular to <i>c</i> axis exhibits good integrity without cracks, and a good layered structure from bulk to surface is observed. However, the post-cycled NNMO shows formation of severe cracks with a thickness about 300 nm (Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0004">4g</a>,<a href="#anie202411618-fig-0004">h</a>). The crystal structure of the exposed new surface after the crack formation is investigated by HRTEM. As shown in Figure S22a, three type of lattice fringes with layer spacings of 2.38 Å, 2.38 Å, and 2.06 Å can be determined to (−1–11), (1–11), and (200) planes of rock-salt structure, respectively, because the FFT pattern is well consistent with simulated SAED pattern of rock-salt structure obtained from the view of [011] (Figure S23). The inverse FFT pattern (Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0004">4i</a>) was used for better reading, which exhibits clear rock-salt structure. However, the relative intact region still shows maintained layered structure but with severe lattice distortion, as confirmed by Figure S22b and Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0004">4j</a>. This result demonstrates that the crack is formation due to the continuous dissolution of metal ions, which is consistent with previous work.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411618-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0039_R_d203336070e1006" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Nano Energy">39</a></sup></span> As for the pristine MAT-NNMO cathode (Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0004">4k</a>,<a href="#anie202411618-fig-0004">l</a>), it also exhibits a perfect layered structure. Interestingly, the post-cycled MAT-NNMO material after 50 cycles only shows a crack depth of approximately 30 nm (Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0004">4m</a>), which is almost 10 % of the post-cycled NNMO sample. In addition, as shown in Figure S24 and Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0004">4n</a>-p, only a little rock-salt species forms, which means the loss of active substance is tiny, resulting in high specific capacity. In order to confirm the loss of active substance, inductively coupled plasma atomic emission spectroscopy (ICP-AES) test for the dissolved metal ions in electrolyte was investigated for both samples after 50 cycles at 0.1 C. As shown in Figure S25, the ICP-AES results manifest that the dissolved metal ions in NNMO cathode are beyond 3 times higher than MAT-NNMO, suggesting the weakened side reaction in MAT-NNMO due to its robust structure. According to the equivalent circuit (Figure S26), the fitted electrochemical impedance spectroscopy (EIS) patterns of both samples after 50 cycles indicate the lower resistance of solid electrolyte film (R<sub>sf</sub>) and charge transfer (<i>R</i><sub>ct</sub>) in MAT-NNMO (Figure S27), which can be attributed to the less formation of rock-salt structure. In short, the large-volume phase transition in NNMO induce several continuous negative impacts: (1) strain-induced intragranular cracks; (2) the cracks expose new surface and facilitate side reaction, leading to severe loss of active substance; (3) the side reaction results in the formation of rock salt and deteriorates the kinetics behavior. Interestingly, the negative impact can be well restricted via the customized multiple rock-forming elements trace doping.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202411618-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/df6890ca-f6fe-4c3d-baf1-d783dea6effb/anie202411618-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/df6890ca-f6fe-4c3d-baf1-d783dea6effb/anie202411618-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/d74e59c7-0fb7-41a1-a785-e97773e7a17b/anie202411618-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/df6890ca-f6fe-4c3d-baf1-d783dea6effb/anie202411618-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202411618-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202411618" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>SEM images of (a) pristine and (b) 50 cycled materials of NNMO. SEM images of (c) pristine and (d) 50 cycled materials of MAT-NNMO. TEM and HRTEM images of (e, f) pristine and (g, h) 50 cycled NNMO. (i, j) The inverse FFT and corresponding filter FFT pattern (inset) collected from two regions of (h). TEM and HRTEM images of (k, l) pristine and (m, n) 50 cycled MAT-NNMO. (o, p) The inverse FFT and corresponding filter FFT pattern (inset) collected from two regions of (n).</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The DFT calculation was used to study the physical properties of these two samples insightfully. The P2−O2 phase transition is induced by the sliding of TMO<sub>2</sub> layer along the <i>a-b</i> planes. Therefore, the energy barrier for the sliding of TMO<sub>2</sub> layer along the <i>a-b</i> planes is the determined factor for estimating the depth of phase transition. As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0005">5a</a>, a pristine MAT-NNMO structure and a structure with one TMO<sub>2</sub> layer sliding 2/3 lattice parameter of a along the <i>a</i> axis were set as initial and final state for the calculation of sliding energy barrier. Similar to MAT-NNMO, the NNMO cathode material has adopted the same calculation method (Figure S28). As a result, Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0005">5b</a> shows that the MAT-NNMO exhibits higher energy barrier (3642 meV) than the NNMO (2987 meV) for the sliding of TMO<sub>2</sub> layer, which indicates that MAT-NNMO is more difficult to undergo a phase transition. The increased sliding energy barrier in MAT-NNMO could be attributed to the trace doping of rock-forming elements of Mg-Al-Ti, which enhances the structure stability due to the entropy stability effect induced high resistance to structural deformation.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411618-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0040_R_d203336070e1073" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">40</a></sup></span> The climbing image nudged elastic band method (CI-NEB) was used to investigate the Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion kinetics.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411618-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0041_R_d203336070e1079" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Adv. Energy Mater.">41</a>, <a href="#anie202411618-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0042_R_d203336070e1079" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - Nat. Commun.">42</a></sup></span> The diffusion pathway in both samples is set as Na<sub>f</sub>-Na<sub>e</sub>-Na<sub>f</sub>, as illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0005">5c</a>,<a href="#anie202411618-fig-0005">d</a>. The obtained results show that the MAT-NNMO exhibits a low energy barrier of 0.16 eV, which is lower than the contrast sample of NNMO (0.33 eV), as depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0005">5e</a>. In short, it can be summarized that the trace doping of Mg-Al-Ti multiple elements increases the sliding energy barrier of TMO<sub>2</sub> layer along the <i>a-b</i> planes and decreases the energy barrier for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion, which effectively improve the cycle and rate performance.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202411618-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/1da6a35b-ca3c-49c4-9e03-576a4785369a/anie202411618-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/1da6a35b-ca3c-49c4-9e03-576a4785369a/anie202411618-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/ae9ce859-aef0-46b3-a25c-5ecbcb6f41dc/anie202411618-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/1da6a35b-ca3c-49c4-9e03-576a4785369a/anie202411618-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202411618-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202411618" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>DFT calculation. (a) The initial and final state for the sliding energy barrier calculation. (b) The obtained sliding energy barrier for both samples. (c, d) The schematic diagram of diffusion pathway for both samples. (e) The calculated energy barriers for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>To further understand the electrochemical reaction process of these two samples, the charge compensation mechanism was thoroughly investigated by adopting X-ray absorption spectroscopy (XAS) and X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS). We first study the potential valence changes of Ni and Mn elements in NNMO sample by XAS measurement. As depicted in Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0006">6a</a>, the Ni K-edge X-ray absorption near-edge structure (XANES) spectrum in pristine NNMO is close to the standard spectrum of NiO reference and exhibits an absorption edge at lower energy compared to LiNiO<sub>2</sub> reference, indicating that the valence of Ni element in pristine NNMO is close to +2.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411618-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0043_R_d203336070e1138" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">43</a></sup></span> The Ni K-edge XANES spectrum shifts toward higher energy and beyond the reference of LiNiO<sub>2</sub>, indicating Ni ion was oxidized approaching +4. During discharge process, the spectrum moves back, revealing the highly reversible Ni redox couples. In addition, the extended X-ray absorption fine structure (EXAFS) spectra at Ni K-edge were also presented to further demonstrate the radial distance evolution. It is well known that the EXAFS spectra in typical layered oxides are characterized by two main features, which are indexed to TM−O in first coordination shell and TM−M in the second coordination shell.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411618-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0044_R_d203336070e1143" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Inorg. Chem.">44</a></sup></span> Obviously, the Ni K-edge EXAFS spectra (Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0006">6b</a>) exhibit similar phenomenon to the XANES results. However, the Mn K-edge XANES spectra in NNMO (Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0006">6c</a>) are almost consistent with the MnO<sub>2</sub> reference at pristine, desodiation, and sodiation state, which indicate that the Mn element is always close to +4 during charge–discharge process.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411618-bib-0045" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0045_R_d203336070e1155" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 45 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">45</a></sup></span> The slightly fluctuation in Mn K-edge EXAFS spectra (Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0006">6d</a>) can be attributed to the changes in local structure. In the same way, as shown in Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0006">6e</a>–<a href="#anie202411618-fig-0006">h</a>, we also find the similar feature in MAT-NNMO electrode, which shows that the redox active center is Ni in MAT-NNMO as well. In order to investigate the detailed contribution of Ni to the capacity during charge–discharge process, the valence of Ni for both samples at different states was obtained from the Ni K-edge XANES spectra. According to the standard reference spectra of Ni<sup>2+</sup> (NiO) and Ni<sup>3+</sup> (LiNiO<sub>2</sub>), the detailed Ni oxidation state is obtained from linear combination analysis.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411618-bib-0046" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0046_R_d203336070e1174" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 46 - Nano Energy">46</a></sup></span> As shown in Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0006">6i</a>, the calculated Ni oxidation state in NNMO varies from 2.318 (pristine) to 3.752 (4.35 V) and final to 2.078 (2.0 V). For the MAT-NNMO sample, the Ni oxidation state also experiences similar changes, from 2.200 (pristine) to 3.830 (4.35 V) and final to 2.125 (2.0 V). However, the Mn valance deduced from the metal oxide references according to the integral method (Figure S29) show almost no changes during charge/discharge process with valence of about 3.90 (Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0006">6j</a>).<span><sup><a href="#anie202411618-bib-0047" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0047_R_d203336070e1184" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 47 - Anal. Bioanal. Chem.">47</a></sup></span> Considering the electrochemical inactive feature of Mg, Al, and Ti, the charge compensation in both samples can only be attributed to Ni and O elements.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411618-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0017_R_d203336070e1187" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Adv. Mater.">17</a>, <a href="#anie202411618-bib-0048" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0048_R_d203336070e1187" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 48 - J. Mater. Chem. A">48</a>, <a href="#anie202411618-bib-0049" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0049_R_d203336070e1187" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 49 - Angew. Chem. Int. Ed.">49</a></sup></span> Therefore, the capacity contribution from the Ni redox can be deduced by how much the valence changes. The theoretical capacities for NNMO and MAT-NNMO are calculated to be 173 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and 177 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. In this content, the capacity contribution from Ni redox in NNMO during charge and discharge process is 122.2 and 142.7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, respectively. In the same way the contribution to the capacity in MAT-NNMO from Ni redox is calculated to 120.6 (charge process) and 126.2 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> (discharge process). The extra capacity is mainly contributed from the oxygen redox above 3.8 V, as confirmed by the O 1s XPS spectra at different states (Figure S30). Hence, the deduced maximum capacity contribution from oxygen redox for NNMO is about 45 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> in time of charge process and only about 7 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> during discharge process, which means poor reversibility of oxygen redox. However, as for the MAT-NNMO, the deduced contribution of oxygen redox to capacity is about 12 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> during charge process and about 14 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> during discharge process. We speculate that the slightly additional capacity at low-voltage region may be ascribed to the Mn reduction, which is very tiny and do not obvious affect the Mn K-edge XANES spectra. Based on the above analyses, as summarized in Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0006">6k</a>,<a href="#anie202411618-fig-0006">l</a>, it is shown that the MAT-NNMO effectively suppresses the oxygen redox activity and improve the Ni redox activity. According to previous report,<span><sup><a href="#anie202411618-bib-0050" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0050_R_d203336070e1214" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 50 - Adv. Mater.">50</a></sup></span> the suppressed oxygen redox is mainly attributed to the competitive coordination mechanism between doping ions−O and Ni−O, especially for Mg ion. The suppressed oxygen redox leads to a mild oxygen oxidation compared to NNMO, which may not result in serious structure instability like traditional strong anion-redox-related material.<span><sup><a href="#anie202411618-bib-0051" class="bibLink tab-link" id="anie202411618-bib-0051_R_d203336070e1217" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 51 - Joule">51</a></sup></span>
            </p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202411618-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/29f20c8d-0368-479e-9f88-954eb73ae4bf/anie202411618-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/29f20c8d-0368-479e-9f88-954eb73ae4bf/anie202411618-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/0dc25363-2fd6-4cf8-b47e-1341154bade5/anie202411618-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/29f20c8d-0368-479e-9f88-954eb73ae4bf/anie202411618-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=anie202411618-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fanie.202411618" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">
                     <p>The charge compensation mechanism. (a–h) Ni and Mn K-edge XAS spectra and corresponding EXAFS spectra for NNMO and MAT-NNMO at different states, respectively. (i, j) The obtained oxidation state of Ni and Mn at different states. (k, l) Decoupled charge compensation mechanism along with the first charge–discharge process.</p></div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="anie202411618-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="anie202411618-sec-0003-title"> Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, a stable cycle of NNMO-based high-voltage cathode was achieved through a customized multiple rock-forming elements trace doping strategy. The Mg-Al-Ti trace doped MAT-NNMO sample exhibits well suppressed phase transition, which reduces the volume strain, consequently restraining the formation of intragranular cracks. In addition, the thoroughly analyses of post-cycled electrode materials from the micron scale to the nanometer scale revealed the failure mechanism of NNMO and relevant improvement mechanism. As illustrated in Figure <a href="#anie202411618-fig-0007">7</a>, the continuous side reactions with the dissolution of transition metals and formation of rock-salt structure significantly reduce the active substance and lower the kinetics, which are the reason of battery failure. In addition, the poor oxygen reversibility is also harmful to the performance. The MAT-NNMO can well increase the sliding energy barrier of TMO<sub>2</sub> layer and decrease the energy barrier for Na<sup>+</sup> diffusion. Impressively, it exhibits a high reversible specific capacity of 140.3 mAh g<sup>−1</sup>, and a good capacity retention of 81.2 % after 200 cycles at 2 C. The MAT-NNMO//HC full cell exhibits a state-of-the-art energy density of 303.3 Wh kg<sup>−1</sup>. This work provides a new concept to customize the high energy, long life, and high-voltage cathode materials for SIBs.
</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="anie202411618-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/9fa65b2e-c1e2-45ed-afb6-50319f146797/anie202411618-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/9fa65b2e-c1e2-45ed-afb6-50319f146797/anie202411618-fig-0007-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/a32998f5-5c4c-4354-b9a2-0a39ebf8e976/anie202411618-fig-0007-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/9fa65b2e-c1e2-45ed-afb6-50319f146797/anie202411618-fig-0007-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

         </section>
         </section>
